![](images/bbd9bdfeae05a1e293df75e7817b1eb695608078f03936ea0e1b0296e7943846.jpg)  
Fig. 3.1. Partially Assembled Island Showing Lower Half of Inconel Inner Core Shell and the Upper Half of the Beryllium Reflector.

for the annular control-safety rod, which contains a mixture of the oxides of the rare-earth elements. The rod is magnetically supported and can be positioned above the horizontal mid-plane for control. It is free to fall below the mid-plane for

reactor shutdown. The outside diameter of the neutron-absorber section of the rod is 1.28 in., and the annulus is $\frac{1}{8}$ in. wide. The density of the neutron-absorber compact is $6.5\mathrm{g/cm}^3$ ; its principal constituents are $\mathrm{Sm}_2\mathrm{O}_3$ (63.8 wt %) and $\mathrm{Gd}_2\mathrm{O}_3$ (26.3 wt %). The support and the driving rod for the neutron source are coaxial with the control rod. The beryllium blocks in the reflector and the island are in an atmosphere of helium; there is no sodium in the system.

The reactor is mounted above a reservoir for the liquid fuel, which is a mixture of the fluorides of sodium, zirconium, and enriched $(93\% \cup^{235})$ uranium. The fuel is transferred to the reactor by applying helium under pressure to the liquid surface in the reservoir; the return to the reservoir is by gravity. The temperature of the system has been raised to $1350^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ by electrical heaters located external to the reflector and the fuel reservoir. The completed assembly is shown in Fig. 3.3.

After an initial test of system operation and leak tightness with an equimolar mixture of NaF and $\mathsf{ZrF}_{4^{\prime}}$ successive increments of $\mathsf{Na}_2\mathsf{UF}_6$ were added until the reactor became critical. The fuel concentration was then 6.30 wt $\%$ (2.87 mole $\%$ ) uranium, and the excess reactivity, determined from the subsequent calibration of the control rod, was about $0.13\% \Delta k / k$ . A measurement of the overall temperature coefficient of reactivity between 1150 and $1350^{\circ}\mathsf{F}$ showed the value to be negative and equal to $2\times 10^{-5}(\Delta k / k) / ^{\circ}\mathsf{F}$ . An increase in the uranium concentration of the fuel from 6.30 to 6.88 wt $\%$ resulted in an increase in reactivity of $1.3\% \Delta k / k$ . The control rod has a value of $1.7\% \Delta k / k$ when inserted to a point 4 in above the mid-plane.

![](images/6c82d0278d065d8258f77f3b5ae71c2161d07db7b2e44b3b4a316274b45e8d98.jpg)  
Fig. 3.2. Outer Core Shell and Partially Assembled Beryllium Reflector of High-Temperature Critical Assembly.

![](images/c97970d81f6b7cdfa1d997278c3f15fea66dbfa6c11a08380c887acb62982386.jpg)  
Fig. 3.3. High-Temperature Critical Assembly of the Reflector-Moderated Circulating-Fuel Reactor.

Part II

MATERIALS RESEARCH

![](images/f3cff10cf7a82635acdc1632aa126d2e41849ab294a52dfaba1d2dee2d04fc92.jpg)

# 4. CHEMISTRY OF REACTOR MATERIALS

W. R. Grimes

Materials Chemistry Division

Phase equilibrium studies were made of the LiF-ZrF4, UF4-ZrF4, NaF-LiF-ZrF4, NaF-LiF-UF4, NaF-RbF-ZrF4-UF4, KF-ZrF4, and NaF-KF-ZrF4 systems and of the BeF2-containing systems NaF-LiF-BeF2, NaF-LiF-BeF2-UF4, NaF-BeF2, and NaF-KF-BeF2. A revised equilibrium diagram for the LiF-ZrF4 system is presented. Additional data were obtained on the solubility of UF3 in BeF2-bearing compositions.

Additional work was done in investigating the equilibrium reduction of $\mathsf{FeF}_2$ by hydrogen in $\mathsf{NaZrF}_5$ , the reduction of $\mathsf{UF}_4$ by structural metals, and the stability of chromium and iron fluorides in molten fluorides. The experimental preparation of $\mathsf{NH}_4\mathsf{SnF}_3$ and of compounds of $\mathsf{ZrF}_4$ with $\mathsf{CrF}_2$ , $\mathsf{NiF}_2$ , and $\mathsf{FeF}_2$ is described. The investigations of the reaction of $\mathsf{UF}_4$ with uranium in alkali fluorides and the reaction of uranium metal with alkali fluorides were continued. Studies of the reactions between molten fluorides and metals included some exploratory work on the equilibrium between sodium-potassium alloy and NaF-KF melts at $800^{\circ}\mathsf{C}$ .

Fuel purification and production research included the investigation of methods for the recovery of contaminated fuel for re-use and the conversion of $\mathsf{ZrO}_2$ to $\mathsf{ZrF}_4$ in the present production equipment. The studies of processing techniques for the purification of $\mathsf{BeF}_2$ -bearing compositions were continued. Efforts to resolve the problems in the procurement of adequate supplies of $\mathsf{ZrF}_4$ are described, as well as improvements in batching and dispensing operations and loading and draining operations.

Potential measurements were made with combinations of several half cells consisting of metal electrodes bathed in solutions of the corresponding metal ion in the molten salt mixture $\mathsf{NaF - ZrF}_4$ . Also, attempts to measure the activity of chromium in Inconel are described. The capillary viscometer was used for viscosity measurements of a mixture of $\mathsf{LiNO}_3$ and $\mathsf{KNO}_3$ . Optical properties and x-ray patterns are presented for recently discovered compounds in fluoride systems: 3LiF-ZrF4, 2LiF-ZrF4, 3LiF-4ZrF4, 2KF·BeF2, KF·BeF2, KF·NaF-ZrF4, 3KF·3NaF-2ZrF4, 3NaF-4ZrF4, and 2NaF·LiF-2BeF2.

Final results obtained for the electrical con

ductance and density of all molten alkali chlorides, bromides, and iodides have been obtained, and conclusions based on the measurements are summarized. Measurements of freezing-point depression are under way. Precise determinations of the self-diffusion coefficients of sodium ion and of nitrate ion in molten sodium nitrate have been completed.

# PHASE EQUILIBRIUM STUDIES OF SYSTEMS CONTAINING $\mathsf{ZrF}_4$ AND/OR $\mathsf{UF}_4$

C.J.Barton F.F.Blankenship

Materials Chemistry Division

H. Insley, Consultant

The System LiF-ZrF<sub>4</sub>

H.A.Friedman R.E.Thoma

R.E.Moore

Materials Chemistry Division

G. D. White, Metallurgy Division

D. L. Stockton, Merck & Co.

A revised phase diagram of the $\mathsf{LiF - ZrF_4}$ system is presented in Fig. 4.l. The original, tentative diagram was based mainly on thermal analysis data. The revised diagram is based on thermal and differential thermal analysis data, high-temperature x-ray diffraction data, and petrographic and x-ray diffraction examination of quenched and slowly cooled samples. The $\mathsf{Li}_3\mathsf{ZrF}_7$ crystals obtained by quenching and by slow cooling were poorly formed, and it is possible that a rapid inversion from a high-temperature crystal modification occurred even during quenching. High-temperature x-ray diffraction data on the compound indicated that the high-temperature modification exists above $470^{\circ}\mathsf{C}$ . Below $470^{\circ}\mathsf{C}$ , the pattern was identical to the room-temperature pattern obtained from a slowly cooled sample which contained the low-temperature form and some of the decomposition products, LiF and $\mathsf{Li}_2\mathsf{ZrF}_6$ . The inversion must occur just above the decomposition temperature.

Quenches of the mixture with 31 mole $\%$ ZrF4

![](images/36720e2843470f27a797fac5b1d7c9dc5e55dc40678768a43868e8a20dc90cf0.jpg)  
Fig. 4.1. Revised Equilibrium Diagram for the System LiF-ZrF<sub>4</sub>.

established the primary phase as $\mathsf{Li}_2\mathsf{ZrF}_6$ and the liquidus temperature was found to be about $590^{\circ}C.$ Recently obtained thermal analysis data showed that the $\mathsf{Li}_2\mathsf{ZrF}_6\mathsf{-Li}_3\mathsf{ZrF}_7$ eutectic melts at about $580^{\circ}C.$ Since $\mathsf{Li}_2\mathsf{ZrF}_6$ is the primary phase of the mixture containing 31 mole $\%$ $\mathsf{ZrF}_{4}$ the $\mathsf{Li}_2\mathsf{ZrF}_6$ eutectic must contain less than 31 mole $\%$ of $\mathsf{ZrF}_4$

No $\mathsf{Li}_3\mathsf{ZrF}_7$ was found in quenched samples containing 33.3 mole $\%$ $\mathsf{ZrF}_4$ over the range 530 to $619^{\circ}\mathsf{C}$ , and thus it was confirmed that $\mathsf{Li}_2\mathsf{ZrF}_6$ melts congruently. The melting point of $\mathsf{Li}_2\mathsf{ZrF}_6$ was found, by thermal analysis, to be about $587^{\circ}\mathsf{C}$ .

Thermal and differential thermal analysis data indicated that a eutectic is formed by the mixture with about 50 mole $\%$ $\mathsf{ZrF}_4$ . The primary phase of the mixture with 48 mole $\%$ $\mathsf{ZrF}_4$ was found, by quenching, to be $\mathsf{Li}_2\mathsf{ZrF}_6$ ; the liquidus temperature was $522^{\circ}\mathsf{C}$ , and the solidus temperature was $508^{\circ}\mathsf{C}$ . The existence of $\mathsf{Li}_2\mathsf{ZrF}_6$ and $\mathsf{Li}_3\mathsf{Zr}_4\mathsf{F}_{19}$

in these samples was confirmed by x-ray diffraction examination. The $\mathsf{Li}_2\mathsf{ZrF}_6\cdot \mathsf{Li}_3\mathsf{Zr}_4\mathsf{F}_{19}$ eutectic appears to contain about 49 mole $\%$ $\mathsf{ZrF}_4$ and to melt at $508^{\circ}C$

The experiments reported previously<sup>1</sup> seemed to narrow down the composition of the unknown phase in the mixture with more than 50 mole % ZrF<sub>4</sub> to either Li<sub>2</sub>Zr<sub>3</sub>F<sub>14</sub> (60 mole % ZrF<sub>4</sub>) or Li<sub>3</sub>Zr<sub>4</sub>F<sub>19</sub> (57.1 mole % ZrF<sub>4</sub>). Other experiments confirmed this conclusion, but no decision between the two formulas could be reached on the basis of the quenching experiments. However, analogy to the NaF-ZrF<sub>4</sub> system indicated Li<sub>3</sub>Zr<sub>4</sub>F<sub>19</sub> to be the more probable, and it has been established in other experiments (described below) that Na<sub>3</sub>Zr<sub>4</sub>F<sub>19</sub> and Li<sub>3</sub>Zr<sub>4</sub>F<sub>19</sub> belong to the same crystal system and form a complete solid-solution series.

Another phase (cubic) has sometimes appeared in quenched samples in the region above 50 mole %

$\mathsf{ZrF}_4$ at temperatures at which liquid would be expected to be present. A differential thermal analysis break at $580^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ was thought at first to be associated with this phase. However, quench growth and well-crystallized $\mathsf{ZrF}_4$ were found in quenched samples containing 57.1, 60, 62.5, 66.7, and 75 mole $\%$ $\mathsf{ZrF}_4$ above $520^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ , the incongruent melting point of $\mathsf{Li}_3\mathsf{Zr}_4\mathsf{F}_{19}$ . There appeared to be no difference in the samples taken above and below $580^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ . It has been decided that the cubic material is either a metastable or a low-temperature phase which sometimes precipitates during quenching. The break at $580^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ was probably due to nonequilibrium conditions.

The liquidus temperature of the mixture with 66.7 mole $\%$ $\mathsf{ZrF}_4$ was found by quenching to be near $695^{\circ}C$ . Thermal analysis data for the mixture with 57.5 mole $\%$ $\mathsf{ZrF}_4$ gave a liquidus temperature of $618^{\circ}C$ .

There is evidence that $\mathsf{Li}_3\mathsf{Zr}_4\mathsf{F}_{19}$ decomposes slowly into $\mathsf{Li}_2\mathsf{ZrF}_6$ and $\mathsf{ZrF}_4$ upon cooling. A relatively pure sample of $\mathsf{Li}_3\mathsf{Zr}_4\mathsf{F}_{19}$ , prepared by heating for 48 hr at $500^{\circ}\mathsf{C}$ , was reheated to $446^{\circ}\mathsf{C}$ for several hours and quenched. The sample then gave a good x-ray pattern for $\mathsf{Li}_2\mathsf{ZrF}_6$ and $\mathsf{ZrF}_4$ , although some $\mathsf{Li}_3\mathsf{Zr}_4\mathsf{F}_{19}$ remained. The decomposition temperature has not been definitely established.

The System $\mathsf{UF}_4\text{-}\mathsf{ZrF}_4$

R.P.Metcalf R.E.Thoma Materials Chemistry Division

The system $\mathsf{UF}_4\text{-}\mathsf{ZrF}_4$ has been studied by using the quenching technique, and it has been analyzed by using petrographic and x-ray diffraction methods. The only crystalline phases which have been observed in this system are solid solutions, $(\mathsf{U},\mathsf{Zr})\mathsf{F}_4$ . There is a minimum melting point of about $765^{\circ}C$ near the composition 23 mole $\%$ $\mathsf{UF}_4 - 77$ mole $\% \mathsf{ZrF}_4$ . It is not yet certain whether there is complete miscibility in the solid phase.

The System NaF-LiF-ZrF<sub>4</sub>

H. E. Friedman R. E. Thoma  
Materials Chemistry Division

When it became apparent that the LiF-ZrF4 binary system contained the previously unidentified incongruently melting compound 3LiF.4ZrF4, which corresponds to the incongruently melting compound 3NaF.4ZrF4 in the NaF-ZrF4 system, a

series of cooling curves was carried out on mixtures of 3LiF.4ZrF and 3NaF.4ZrF. A continuous gradation of optical properties from 3NaF.4ZrF (n = 1.420, n = 1.432; biaxial positive; 2V = 30 deg) to 3LiF.4ZrF (n = 1.463, n = 1.473; biaxial positive; 2V = 20 to 30 deg) gave strong indication of a complete solid-solution series. The primary phase along the join was ZrF, and the liquidus temperatures increased steadily from 550°C for 3NaF.4ZrF to 618°C for 3LiF.4ZrF. The solidus temperatures, which corresponded to the incongruent melting temperatures of the solid-solution series, exhibited a minimum at 475°C for the mixture with about 25 mole % LiF. The eutectic in the compatibility triangle formed by 6NaF.7ZrF, 2LiF.4ZrF, and the 3(Na,Li)F.4ZrF solid solution was found to be NaF-LiF-ZrF (25-29-46 mole %) and to melt at 445°C.

An intensive study of the NaF-LiF-ZrF-UF4quaternary system was interrupted when it was learned that no improvement in viscosity resulted when the mixtures contained relatively high LiF and low $\mathsf{ZrF}_4$ contents. At high zirconium concentrations, there was a marked reduction in melting point which was, however, accompanied by unusually high $\mathsf{ZrF}_4$ vapor pressures.

The System NaF-LiF-UF

H.E.Friedman R.E.Thoma

Materials Chemistry Division

R. E. Cleary

Pratt & Whitney Aircraft

T. N. McVay

Consultant

The compound LiF forms nearly complete quasi-binary systems with each of the NaF-UF4 compounds: 3NaF-UF4, 2NaF-UF4, and 7NaF-6UF4. Since 2NaF-UF4 is just barely an incongruity melting compound, the LiF-2NaF-UF4 join departs from true quasi-binary behavior when the LiF content is low. Saddle eutectics are found on the LiF-3NaF-UF4 join that contain 20 mole % LiF and melt at $480^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ , on the LiF-2NaF-UF4 join that contain 31 mole % LiF and melt at $510^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ , and on the LiF-6NaF-7UF4 join that contain 39 mole % LiF and melt at $490^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ . However, the mixtures that are shown on the diagram to have low melting points contain too much UF4 for them to be of use as circulating reactor fuels.

The join between $6\mathrm{NaF}\cdot 7\mathrm{UF}_4$ and $6\mathrm{LiF}\cdot 7\mathrm{UF}_4$ appears to form a side of a compatibility triangle, and so does the join between $6\mathrm{NaF}\cdot 7\mathrm{UF}_4$ and $3\mathrm{LiF}\cdot \mathrm{UF}_4$ . These two joins, along with the quasi binaries described in the foregoing paragraph, define the important compatibility triangles in the portion of the ternary mixture that contains 47 mole $\%$ or less, $\mathrm{UF}_4$ . In the region of $\mathrm{UF}_4$ concentrations above 50 mole $\%$ there is at least one ternary compound; the high $\mathrm{UF}_4$ -content portion of the diagram is not yet understood, but the first indications are that $\mathrm{UF}_4$ is a compatible phase over a surprisingly small area.

As a result of these studies it appears that some complications, previously unrecognized, appear in the $\mathsf{LiF - UF_4}$ binary system. In addition to the $3\mathrm{LiF - UF}_4$ and $\mathsf{LiF - 2UF_4}$ compounds previously observed, there is another compound, $7\mathrm{LiF - 6UF_4}$ . On cooling curves at compositions near the single eutectic (28 mole $\%$ UF), the temperature may rise and fall 5 to $15^{\circ}C$ two or three times after the eutectic halt is under way. This abnormal behavior seems to be due to an exothermic decomposition of $3\mathrm{LiF - UF_4}$ at about the eutectic temperature, along with a tendency for the melt to undercool and then rapidly decompose.

The System NaF-RbF-ZrF4-UF4

H. A. Friedman R. E. Thoma  
Materials Chemistry Division

Preliminary studies of some systems containing RbF and $\mathsf{ZrF}_4$ were made in an attempt to obtain materials with low viscosities for possible use as reactor fuels. Previous studies of the RbF-ZrF4 binary system had shown low melting points only for mixtures with moderately high (greater than 40 mole %) $\mathsf{ZrF}_4$ concentrations; 3RbF-ZrF4 melts at $900^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ . Consequently, low-melting mixtures containing RbF and $\mathsf{UF}_4$ with 20 to 40 mole % $\mathsf{ZrF}_4$ necessarily contain a fourth component.

The RbF available, at present, contains about 20 mole $\%$ of KF, and therefore all studies reported here were made with RbF containing that amount of KF. It was anticipated that the KF would substitute isomorphically for RbF and that the phase diagrams would not appear to involve the KF component. Unfortunately, this proved not

to be the case. For example, $3\text{RbF} \cdot Z\text{rF}_4$ formed preferentially, and, if additional KF were present, an excess of KF remained. In low-melting parts of the NaF-(Rb,K)F-ZrF₄ system, unexpected phases made their appearance, and therefore quasi binaries could not be established. A join at 47 mole % ZrF₄ appeared to pass through the phase fields of two ternary compounds and to give the lowest melting points at the high RbF-content end. This behavior, coupled with lack of information regarding the similar NaF-KF-ZrF₄ system, caused the postponement of a systematic study of the NaF-RbF-ZrF₄ diagram, pending results from viscosity measurements on an RbF-ZrF₄-UF₄ fuel mixture and the procurement of better RbF.

In an effort to find a suitable fuel mixture for viscosity measurements, a constant 4 mole % of $\mathsf{UF}_4$ was added to the RbF-ZrF $_4$ binary over a range of compositions from 55 to 45 mole % RbF. All these mixtures appeared to melt between 410 and $420^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ and to give no detectable heat effects above this range. This result led to the suspicion, later confirmed, that small amounts of precipitates with a very low temperature coefficient of solubility were escaping detection. From the middle of this range of apparently low-melting mixtures, the composition RbF-ZrF $_4$ -UF $_4$ (48-48-4 mole %) was chosen as a preparation for viscosity measurements. The purified mixture was cooled to $450^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ to precipitate any solidified crystals before transfer; however, the transfer was carried out through heated lines and a heated filter, so the flowing melt was heated to $575^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ . Crystals of $(\mathsf{Zr},\mathsf{U})\mathsf{F}_4$ solid solution were found in the heel remaining in the purification vessel but not in the filtered product. Hence, $(\mathsf{Zr},\mathsf{U})\mathsf{F}_4$ was presumed to be the primary phase; it is possible that the $(\mathsf{Zr},\mathsf{U})\mathsf{F}_4$ precipitated incompletely in the 15-min equilibration period at $450^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ , since small particles were subsequently observed in the melted product at temperatures above $500^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ . However, petrographic examination of the solidified melts in which particles had been observed while they were molten showed the presence of significant amounts of oxyfluoride.

Viscosity measurements below $600^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ were not satisfactory because of these particles, but the values obtained above $600^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ were comparable with those obtained, to date, for satisfactory fuel mixtures (see Sec. 7, "Heat Transfer and Physical Properties"). All the work described here was

carried out with RbF containing about 20 mole % KF.

# The System KF-ZrF<sub>4</sub>

H. A. Friedman R. E. Thoma

Materials Chemistry Division

T. N. McVay

Consultant

The system $\mathsf{KF - ZrF}_4$ is being studied in an attempt to obtain background information regarding the rather similar $\mathsf{RbF - ZrF}_4$ system and to check and refine techniques for handling these hygroscopic materials. Cooling curves obtained for ten stirred $\mathsf{KF - ZrF}_4$ melts with compositions in the range 33.3 to 59 mole $\%$ $\mathsf{ZrF}_4$ were in general agreement with the phase diagram previously published. Thermal breaks in the range 45 to 50 mole $\%$ $\mathsf{ZrF}_4$ indicate that there are two forms of $\mathsf{KZrF}_5$ , one of which is metastable.

# The System NaF-KF-ZrF<sub>4</sub>

H.A.Friedman R.E.Thoma

Materials Chemistry Division

Preliminary studies indicate that the NaF-KF-ZrF $_4$ system is much more complex than either the NaF-ZrF $_4$ -UF $_4$ system or the NaF-LiF-UF $_4$ system. Neither of these latter systems shows ternary compounds; in contrast, the NaF-KF-ZrF $_4$ system shows at least five such compounds. One compound has the composition NaF-KF-ZrF $_4$ , and another has the composition 3NaF-3KF-2ZrF $_4$ . Although the latter compound forms a compatibility triangle with NaF and K $_3$ ZrF $_7$ , it cannot form a quasi binary with K $_3$ ZrF $_7$ , Na $_3$ ZrF $_7$ , or NaF, because it apparently melts incongruity at about $755^{\circ}$ C to K $_3$ ZrF $_7$ and liquid. Cooling curves on the Na $_3$ ZrF $_7$ -K $_3$ ZrF $_7$ join show a minimum in the liquidus temperatures at about $800^{\circ}$ C for the composition with about 15 mole % KF; the liquidus temperatures then rise gradually to about $918^{\circ}$ C for the compound K $_3$ ZrF $_7$ and about $850^{\circ}$ C for the compound Na $_3$ ZrF $_7$ . Since a slowly cooled melt of a composition midway between 3NaF-3KF-2ZrF $_4$ and NaF-KF-ZrF $_4$ consists of only these phases, it can be concluded that this join is a common base of two, as yet undetermined, compatibility triangles.

Eight melts were made along the 33.3 mole $\%$ $\mathsf{ZrF}_4$ join of compositions containing from 15 to 50 mole $\%$ KF and NaF as the remainder. Although all these compositions contained the compound NaF.KF.ZrF $^4$ as one of the phases in the completely crystallized preparation, liquidus-temperature relations indicate that the compound melts incongruently and that therefore there is no quasi binary along this join.

Melting relations and phase compositions of six preparations along the 47 mole $\%$ $\mathsf{ZrF}_4$ join indicate that the mixture with 47 mole $\%$ $\mathsf{ZrF}_4$ also is not a quasi binary, because most of the compositions consist of three solid phases, including a new ternary compound of undetermined composition that is probably near NaF-2KF-3ZrF. The minimum liquidus temperature along this join is about $420^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ for a mixture with about 35 mole $\%$ KF. A search in this general area has indicated that there is a eutectic, with the approximate composition 38 mole $\%$ KF, 21 mole $\%$ NaF, 41 mole $\%$ ZrF, which melts at about $400^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ .

# PHASE EQUILIBRIUM STUDIES OF SYSTEMS

# CONTAINING BeF

C. J. Barton and F. F. Blankenship

L.M.Bratcher R.J.Sheil   
B.H.Clampitt R.E.Thoma

Materials Chemistry Division

R. E. Cleary, Pratt & Whitney Aircraft

T. N. McVay, Consultant

# The System NaF-LiF-BeF

Thermal analysis data were obtained with a number of compositions within a wedge-shaped area in the ternary diagram having the $\mathrm{LiF - Na_2BeF_4}$ eutectic (16 mole $\%$ LiF) at the apex and $\mathrm{Na_2BeF_4}$ and $\mathrm{NaF - BeF_2}$ (70-30 mole $\%$ ) at the other corners. The available data indicate that acceptably low melting points can be obtained with low LiF concentration by moving into the ternary system along the drainage path leading from the $\mathrm{NaF - Na_2BeF_4}$ eutectic (approximately 31 mole $\%$ $\mathrm{BeF_2}$ ; melting point, $570^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ ) toward the $\mathrm{LiF - Na_2BeF_4}$ eutectic. Further, viscosity data for $\mathrm{BeF_2}$ -bearing mixtures indicate that, in order to obtain a melt with a kinematic viscosity as low as that of the ARE-type fuel (2.82 centistokes at $600^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ and 1.34 centistokes at $800^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ ), the $\mathrm{BeF_2}$ content of the melt

must not be greater than 30 mole $\%$ to assure complexing of $\mathsf{BeF}_2$ as $\mathsf{BeF}_4 = -\mathsf{i}$ ions in the melt. If an NaF-LiF-BeF2 mixture is desired, it also appears that the LiF content will probably have to be less than 10 mole $\%$ One composition that meets these requirements is the mixture NaF-LiF- $\mathsf{BeF}_2$ (63.5-7.5-29 mole $\%$ ), which has a melting point of about $525^{\circ}C.$ It is expected that the viscosity of this mixture will be determined in the near future.

The low-melting ternary mixture NaF-LiF-BeF $_2$ (27-35-38 mole %), reported previously on the basis of cooling curve data to melt at $325 \pm 10^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ , was found to melt at $339 \pm 5^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ by the use of differential thermal analysis techniques. The former value was low because of undercooling. This mixture has been reported to have kinematic viscosities that are too high - 3.85 centistokes at $600^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ and 1.47 centistokes at $800^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ - to be considered competitive with those of the ARE fuel but low enough to be of interest for a reactor design requiring a substantially lower melting point fuel than can be obtained with known $\mathsf{ZrF}_4$ -base compositions.

Liquidus temperatures were determined for several compositions by differential thermal analysis of samples cooled rapidly from $675^{\circ}C$ to room temperature in order to avoid the segregation of the crystalline components that apparently occurs on slow cooling. The composition NaF-LiF-BeF $_2$ $(50 - 16\%)$ showed a liquidus temperature of $478^{\circ}C$ and a solidus temperature, determined from heating curves, of $329^{\circ}C$ . The mixture NaF-LiF-BeF $_2$ (64-5-31 mole %) showed a melting point of $545^{\circ}C$ and a freezing point of $555^{\circ}C$ . An unpurified melt of this composition froze at $535^{\circ}C$ . Most mixtures in this system have exhibited much lower freezing points than melting points because of the prevalence of undercooling, but the 64-5-31 mole % mixture appears to be exceptional in this respect.

The equimolar composition NaF-LiF-BeF $_2$ , previously investigated by Jahn, $^9$ was studied by using quenching and differential thermal analysis techniques. The latter technique gave a value of $434^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ for the liquidus temperature, as compared

with the approximate value of $460^{\circ}C$ reported by Jahn. A crystalline phase having the x-ray pattern described by Jahn for the $\mathrm{NaLiBeF_4}$ compound prepared from aqueous solution was observed in samples of this composition quenched at temperatures ranging from 406 to $562^{\circ}C$ . At the lower temperature, LiF and $\mathrm{Na}_2\mathrm{LiBe}_2\mathrm{F}_7$ were also present, while in samples quenched at temperatures above the liquidus temperature, quench growth was observed petrographically. Jahn's failure to obtain $\mathrm{NaLiBeF_4}$ from melted samples by quenching at temperatures below $550^{\circ}C$ , which he ascribed to formation of $\mathrm{Be}_2\mathrm{F}_7^{3 - }$ ions in the liquid, was more likely due to poor quenching technique. Solid-phase decomposition of $\mathrm{NaLiBeF_4}$ to give LiF and $\mathrm{Na}_2\mathrm{LiBe}_2\mathrm{F}_7$ at $225^{\circ}C$ , as reported by Jahn, was confirmed by the quenching experiments.

# The System NaF-LiF-BeF $_2$ -UF $_4$

Investigations of the NaF-LiF-BeF $_2$ -UF $_4$ system were confined to liquidus temperature determinations with mixtures containing 2.5 mole $\%$ UF $_4$ , which gives approximately the same concentration of UF $_4$ (in g/cm $^3$ ) at $800^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ as that of NaF-ZrF $_4$ . UF $_4$ (50-46-4 mole $\%$ ). Various techniques, including thermal analysis, differential thermal analysis, filtration, and visual observation, were used. The results are summarized in Table 4.1.

The first mixture listed in Table 4.1 was previously reported to have a liquidus temperature of $465 \pm 5^{\circ} \mathrm{C}$ , as determined by visual observation. The filtration experiment demonstrated that the small, high-temperature thermal effects observed on cooling curves were spurious.

Analyses of the filtrates obtained with the 64-5-31 mole $\%$ mixture showed an increasing concentration of uranium from 540 to $520^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ , which indicated that a nonuranium phase was precipitating in this temperature range. The filtration of the 27-35-38 mole $\%$ mixture showed that 9.65 wt $\%$ uranium, equivalent to about 1.8 mole $\%$ UF $_{4'}$ was in solution at $335^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ .

# The System NaF-BeF2-UF4

The reported low viscosity of the NaF-BeF $_2$ (70-30 mole %) composition<sup>10</sup> prompted interest in determining liquidus temperatures of mixtures formed by adding 2.5 mole % UF $_4$ to NaF-BeF $_2$

mixtures of approximately the 70-30 mole % composition. Differential thermal analysis data obtained with rapidly cooled samples of the mixture NaF-BeF $_2$ -UF $_4$ (68.25-29.25-2.5 mole %) indicated a probable liquidus temperature of $579 \pm 5^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ . Filtration data obtained with the mixture NaF-BeF $_2$ -UF $_4$ (66.8-30.7-2.5 mole %) showed the liquidus temperature to be below $562^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ . The highest thermal effect observed on cooling curves with the latter composition was at about $555^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ . There appears to be little hope of obtaining an acceptable liquidus temperature with a ternary mixture of this type containing 2.5 mole % UF $_4$ and 30 mole %, or less, BeF $_2$ .

# The System KF-BeF

Thermal analysis data recently obtained with purified mixtures in the $\mathsf{KF - BeF}_2$ system showed slightly higher melting points than those obtained by C. J. Barton and co-workers in investigations of unpurified melts. In general, however, the thermal analysis data obtained recently agreed with the earlier data within the accuracy claimed for the determinations $(\pm 10^{\circ}\mathrm{C})$ . Two compounds have been observed to date: $\mathsf{K}_2\mathsf{BeF}_4$ , which melts congruently at $788 \pm 5^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ , and $\mathsf{KBeF}_3$ , which apparently melts incongruity at $390^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ and gives $\mathsf{K}_2\mathsf{BeF}_4$ and liquid. The thermal analysis investigation, together with solid-phase studies of the slowly cooled melts, is continuing, and it is expected that a partial phase diagram covering the region 0 to 60 mole $\%$ $\mathsf{BeF}_2$ will be completed in the near future.

# The System NaF-KF-BeF $_2$

Thermal analysis data on about 40 unpurified melts in the NaF-KF-BeF $_2$ system were obtained previously. $^{11}$ The incomplete investigation showed

rather high liquidus temperatures, as compared with those of the NaF-LiF-BeF $_2$ system, and no promising fuel carrier compositions were formulated as a result of these studies. Recent interest in obtaining a BeF $_2$ -base fuel carrier with low kinematic viscosity prompted a reinvestigation of the NaF-KF-BeF $_2$ system. Thermal analysis data obtained with ternary compositions prepared from purified binary mixtures have served to confirm the earlier indications of high liquidus temperatures in this system, particularly for the mixtures containing 33 mole %, or less, BeF $_2$ . The mixtures containing 33 mole %, or less, BeF $_2$ are considered to be the most promising mixtures from the viscosity standpoint. Preliminary results of petrographic and x-ray diffraction examinations of a number of slowly cooled melts show that compositions on the NaF-K $_2$ BeF $_4$ join contain only these two components. Thermal analysis data for this system, shown in Fig. 4.2, indicate that it is a simple eutectic system, with the eutectic containing about 22.5 mole % NaF and having a melting point of $635 \pm 5^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ . The thermal effect observed at $690^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ with K $_2$ BeF $_4$ and the mixture containing 10 mole % NaF probably indicates the existence of a K $_2$ BeF $_4$ solid-phase transition. Examination of mixtures containing 33.3 mole % BeF $_2$ indicates the probability of two ternary compounds on, or near, this join. Possible formulas of these compounds are NaKBeF $_4$ and Na $_3$ K(BeF $_4$ ) $_2$ . An increase in cell size of the phase believed to be NaKBeF $_4$ was noted with mixtures richer in KF content. Therefore there is a possibility of solid-solution formation between K $_2$ BeF $_4$ and the ternary compound.

![](images/44548f70fa89ecfd4237f806944baaef80957659ac4d135cba9bb4452a4e8dd9.jpg)  
Fig. 4.2. Thermal Data for the System NaF-K $_2$ BeF $_4$ .

# Solubility of $\mathsf{UF}_3$ in $\mathsf{BeF}_2$ -Bearing Compositions

Data on the solubility of $\mathsf{UF}_3$ in a number of $\mathsf{BeF}_2$ -bearing compositions were reported earlier. The composition (in mole %) designated 69 NaF-31 $\mathsf{BeF}_2$ in the previous work was actually 69 LiF-31 $\mathsf{BeF}_2$ . All the earlier data were obtained with nickel filters. In view of the greater stability of $\mathsf{UF}_3$ dissolved in LiF-NaF in copper containers (discussed below in this section under the heading "Reaction of $\mathsf{UF}_4$ with Uranium in Alkali Fluorides"), it seemed desirable to see whether more consistent data could be obtained in copper apparatus equipped with a bronze filter medium. The data obtained are shown in Table 4.2, together with some additional data obtained with LiF-BeF $_2$ mixtures in nickel apparatus. The uranium additions were made with $\mathsf{UF}_3$ and uranium metal or by adding large excesses of uranium metal to $\mathsf{UF}_4$ -bearing melts.

It is difficult to draw definite conclusions from such scattered data, but it is apparent that alloying of uranium with the nickel filters and containers

was not the sole cause of the poor reproducibility of the $\mathsf{UF}_3$ -solubility data obtained in nickel apparatus. The large amount of tetravalent uranium found in all the filtrates, regardless of valence form of the uranium added to the solvent, could be due to oxidizing impurities in the melt, disproportionation of $\mathsf{UF}_3$ , or a reaction between $\mathsf{UF}_3$ and the alkali fluoride or beryllium fluoride in the melt. Further experimentation will be required to determine the controlling factor in the production of $\mathsf{UF}_4$ in $\mathsf{BeF}_2$ -bearing compositions.

# CHEMICAL REACTIONS IN MOLTEN SALTS

L.G.Overholser G.M.Watson

Materials Chemistry Division

Equilibrium Reduction of $\mathsf{FeF}_2$ by $\mathsf{H}_2$ in $\mathsf{NaZrF}_5$

C. M. Blood

Materials Chemistry Division

An apparent equilibrium constant (mole fraction of dissolved species used as activity) of 5.7 at $800^{\circ}C$ was previously reported $^{14}$ for the reaction

$$
F e F _ {2} + H _ {2} \rightleftharpoons F e ^ {o} + 2 H F
$$

TABLE 4.2. SOLUBILITY OF ${\mathrm{{UF}}}_{3}$ IN ${\mathrm{{BeF}}}_{2}$ -BEARING COMPOSITIONS   

<table><tr><td rowspan="2">Solvent Composition</td><td rowspan="2">Temperature (°C)</td><td rowspan="2">Container Material</td><td rowspan="2">Uranium Added</td><td colspan="2">Analysis of Filtrate (wt %)</td></tr><tr><td>U3+</td><td>Total U</td></tr><tr><td rowspan="7">LiF·BeF2(69-31 mole %)</td><td>700</td><td>Nickel</td><td>UF3+ U</td><td>2.79</td><td>4.80</td></tr><tr><td>700</td><td>Nickel</td><td>UF4+ U</td><td>3.38</td><td>5.66</td></tr><tr><td>800</td><td>Nickel</td><td>UF3+ U</td><td>8.35</td><td>14.1</td></tr><tr><td>800</td><td>Nickel</td><td>UF4+ U</td><td>8.28</td><td>12.7</td></tr><tr><td>600</td><td>Copper</td><td>UF4+ U</td><td>0.46</td><td>3.12</td></tr><tr><td>700</td><td>Copper</td><td>UF3+ U</td><td>3.09</td><td>5.34</td></tr><tr><td>800</td><td>Copper</td><td>UF4+ U</td><td>5.02</td><td>12.7</td></tr><tr><td rowspan="6">NaF·BeF2(70-30 mole %)</td><td>600</td><td>Copper</td><td>UF3+ U</td><td>0.40</td><td>2.50</td></tr><tr><td>600</td><td>Copper</td><td>UF4+ U</td><td>0.19</td><td>7.05</td></tr><tr><td>700</td><td>Copper</td><td>UF3+ U</td><td>2.75</td><td>5.24</td></tr><tr><td>700</td><td>Copper</td><td>UF4+ U</td><td>4.82</td><td>10.1</td></tr><tr><td>800</td><td>Copper</td><td>UF3+ U</td><td>10.1</td><td>14.7</td></tr><tr><td>800</td><td>Copper</td><td>UF4+ U</td><td>12.2</td><td>19.1</td></tr></table>

when the materials were contained in a mild steel system. This value was obtained by passing hydrogen at different gas flow rates through the salt mixture containing $\mathsf{FeF}_2$ and measuring the corresponding partial pressure of HF in the effluent. An approach to the equilibrium partial pressure of HF was made by extrapolation to the slowest flow rate. As has been stated before,15 the extrapolation does not appear to be satisfactory.

In order to avoid this extrapolation, measurements made by using an equilibration technique, previously described,[16] have been continued. The method consists in bubbling a mixture of HF and $\mathsf{H}_2$ through the melt containing $\mathsf{FeF}_2$ in contact with iron until the HF concentrations of the influent and effluent streams are identical. At this time the concentration of $\mathsf{FeF}_2$ is determined by chemical analysis of samples of the melt withdrawn through a filtering tube.

The results of the most careful measurements performed to date by this method show $K_{x} = 5.2$ at a concentration of $\mathsf{FeF}_2$ corresponding to 6490 ppm

of iron. From all apparent indications, the approach to equilibrium in this instance was better than that for any previous measurement obtained by this technique. Unfortunately, the existence of equilibrium cannot be demonstrated with certainty, since the present equipment cannot provide a gaseous influent of constant composition for long periods of time. An alternate method of formation of $\mathsf{H}_2$ -HF mixtures of constant composition is presently being studied.

# Reduction of $\mathsf{UF}_4$ by Structural Metals

J.D.Redman

C. F. Weaver

Materials Chemistry Division

The techniques used for measuring the reduction of $\mathsf{UF}_4$ by $\mathsf{Cr}^{\circ}$ or $\mathsf{Fe}^{\circ}$ in molten fluorides were described in earlier reports.17 Equilibrium data for these reactions when the NaF $\cdot$ ZrF $_4$ compound,18

the NaF-LiF-KF eutectic, $^{19}$ or the NaF-ZrF $_4$ (53-47 mole %) mixture $^{20}$ was used as a reaction medium have also been presented. These studies have been continued with experiments in which NaF-LiF-ZrF $_4$ (22-55-23 mole %) was used as the reaction medium. A study of the reduction of UF $_4$ by vanadium metal with the NaF-LiF-KF eutectic as the solvent has been started, but no analytical data are yet available. Similar studies, planned for the near future, with metallic niobium, tantalum, and tungsten will be conducted in an attempt to demonstrate whether or not these metals are compatible with UF $_4$ dissolved in the NaF-LiF-KF eutectic at $800^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ .

The results of some studies of the reduction of $\mathsf{UF}_4$ by $\mathsf{Cr}^{\circ}$ at 600 and $800^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ with NaF-LiF-ZrF $_4$ (22-55-23 mole %) as the reaction medium are given in Table 4.3. In all these experiments 2 g of hy

drogen-fired chromium was reacted with 11.4 wt $\%$ UF (2.5 mole $\%$ ) dissolved in approximately $30\mathrm{g}$ of the NaF-LiF-ZrF mixture contained in nickel.

The averages for the chromium values given in Table 4.3 - 570 and 750 ppm at 600 and $800^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ , respectively - are the lowest found for any of the reaction media studied. For comparison, chromium values at 600 and $800^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ in $\mathrm{NaF\cdot ZrF_4}$ were 2250 and 2550 ppm; in $\mathrm{NaF\cdot ZrF_4}$ (53-47 mole %), 1750 and 2150 ppm; in $\mathrm{NaF\cdot LiF\cdot KF}$ (11.5-46.5-42 mole %), 1100 and 2700 ppm. Although the $\mathrm{UF_4}$ concentration was somewhat lower (2.5 compared with 4.0 mole %) in the recent experiments than in the previous experiments, this variable can account for only a small part of the difference found in chromium content. It seems more probable that the most important factor, for the $\mathrm{ZrF_4}$ -containing mixtures, is that with a decrease in $\mathrm{ZrF_4}$ concentration there is also a decrease in the activity of the $\mathrm{UF_4}$ because of increased complexing by the alkali fluorides. The results cannot be compared with those obtained in the NaF-LiF-KF eutectic, because virtually all the chromium is present as the trivalent form in the eutectic. As reported below, a large fraction of the chromium is divalent in the NaF-LiF-ZrF_4 mixture.

Data for the reaction of $\mathsf{UF}_4$ with $\mathsf{Fe}^{\circ}$ in nickel containers at 600 and $800^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ with NaF-LiF-ZrF $_4$ (22-55-23 mole %) as the reaction medium are given in Table 4.4. In all these runs, 2 g of hydrogen-fired iron wire and approximately 30 g of the solvent containing 11.4 wt % (2.5 mole %) $\mathsf{UF}_4$ were employed.

The equilibrium iron values reported in Table 4.4 fall in the same general range as those found in the other reaction media. The one difference to be noted is that the iron values given in Table 4.4 are slightly higher at 800 than at $600^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ , whereas in the $\mathrm{NaF - ZrF_4}$ mixtures and the NaF-LiF-KF eutectic slightly higher values were observed at 600 than at $800^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ .

From the results given in Tables 4.8 and 4.9, it is seen that roughly $80\%$ of the $\mathsf{FeF}_2$ added remains as $\mathsf{Fe}^{2+}$ , with the remainder apparently being oxidized to $\mathsf{Fe}^{3+}$ , although no logical mechanism for the oxidation can be postulated. From the results for the runs in which $\mathsf{FeF}_3$ was added, it is evident, from the increase in the nickel content of the system, that the nickel container reduces $\mathsf{Fe}^{3+}$ to $\mathsf{Fe}^{2+}$ . No satisfactory balance between $\mathsf{Fe}^{2+}$ and $\mathsf{Ni}^{2+}$ is shown by these data; it is therefore possible that the reaction is not the simple one proposed. Some rather extensive experimental work would be required to obtain an insight into the possible equilibria involved.

# Experimental Preparation of Pure Fluorides

B.J.Stum

Materials Chemistry Division

Preparation of $\mathsf{NH}_4\mathsf{SnF}_3$ In order to produce about $500g$ of $\mathsf{NH}_4\mathsf{SnF}_3$ essentially free of chloride ion, stannous hydroxide was precipitated by the addition of aqueous ammonia to a hot aqueous solution of $\mathsf{SnCl}_2$ in the presence of $\mathsf{Sn}^{\circ}$ The precipitate was washed by decantation, and the raffinate was removed by centrifugation. After dissolution of the precipitate in aqueous HF, the precipitation, washing, and centrifugation were repeated. The precipitate was then treated with the stoichiometric quantity of $\mathsf{NH}_4\mathsf{HF}_2$ and was dissolved in the minimum quantity of aqueous HF. Crystals of $\mathsf{NH}_4\mathsf{SnF}_3$ were recovered by partial evaporation of this solution.

Compounds of $\mathbf{ZrF_4}$ with $\mathbf{CrF}_2$ , $\mathbf{NiF}_2$ , and $\mathbf{FeF}_2$ . Evidence from various experiments has indicated that the simple fluorides of chromium, iron, or nickel are not the primary phase when solutions of these materials in NaF-ZrF<sub>4</sub> mixtures are cooled; also, petrographic and x-ray examinations of such cooled melts show the solid phases recovered to be quite similar. It was believed that primary phases were complex compounds of $\mathbf{ZrF}_4$ with the structural metal fluorides. Accordingly, $\mathbf{ZrF}_4$ was heated at $950^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ in sealed nickel capsules with each of the compounds $\mathbf{CrF}_3$ , $\mathbf{CrF}_2$ , $\mathbf{FeF}_2$ , and $\mathbf{NiF}_2$ in 2:1, 1:1, and 1:2 stoichiometric proportions. The $\mathbf{CrF}_3$ did not react with $\mathbf{ZrF}_4$ , and no evidence of compound formation was observed in the three proportions tested. All the $\mathbf{NiF}_2$ - $\mathbf{ZrF}_4$ fusions showed compound formation. When 2 moles of $\mathbf{NiF}_2$ per mole of $\mathbf{ZrF}_4$ was used, the compound and excess $\mathbf{NiF}_2$ were observed; when the molar

ratio was reversed, $\mathsf{ZrF}_4$ and the compound appeared. The compound seemed to be $\mathsf{NiF}_2\cdot \mathsf{ZrF}_4,$ and the constancy of cell size with composition of the mixture indicated very limited or no solid solution of either $\mathsf{NiF}_2$ or $\mathsf{ZrF}_4$ in the compound.

The $\mathsf{ZrF}_4\cdot \mathsf{FeF}_2$ fusions showed a compound that was isomorphous with $\mathsf{NiF}_2\cdot \mathsf{ZrF}_4$ but had a slightly larger cell size. The compound $\mathsf{FeF}_2\cdot \mathsf{ZrF}_4$ , however, unlike its $\mathsf{NiF}_2\cdot \mathsf{ZrF}_4$ counterpart, forms solid solutions with $\mathsf{ZrF}_4$ ; the cell size of the compound appeared to be lowered by the $\mathsf{ZrF}_4$ . When the $\mathsf{FeF}_2$ was in excess (2 moles of $\mathsf{FeF}_2$ to 1 of $\mathsf{ZrF}_4$ ), an additional phase, as yet unidentified, was observed; the unchanged cell size of the $\mathsf{FeF}_2\cdot \mathsf{ZrF}_4$ present indicated virtually no solid solution of these compounds.

Petrographic examination indicated that the compound $\mathrm{CrF}_2\cdot \mathrm{ZrF}_4$ existed and that it was apparently isomorphous with its $\mathrm{FeF}_2$ and $\mathrm{NiF}_2$ counterparts. No x-ray diffraction data and no observations of solid solution formation are yet available. Because of the value of these studies to the interpretation of some electrochemical studies (also reported in this section), these studies will be extended to include systems containing NaF, as well as $\mathrm{ZrF}_4$ .

# Reaction of UF<sub>4</sub> with Uranium in Alkali Fluorides

C.J.Barton

B. H. Clampitt

Materials Chemistry Division

It was previously reported[21] that the reduction of $\mathsf{UF}_4$ in the NaF-LiF (40-60 mole %) eutectic with metallic uranium was more nearly complete in copper than in nickel apparatus. In those experiments the copper apparatus was equipped with a filter medium of bronze (82% Cu, 16% Sn, 2% Zn). The experiments were recently repeated, with a filter medium of pure copper being used; the data from these experiments are presented in Table 4.10. The higher $\mathsf{UF}_3 / \mathsf{UF}_4$ ratios obtained in these experiments suggest that some disproportionation of $\mathsf{UF}_3$ occurred on contact of the melt with the bronze filters.

However, when the reduction of $\mathsf{UF}_4$ with excess $U^{\circ}$ was carried out with the NaF-KF-LiF eutectic (11.5-42-46.5 mole %) as the solvent, the reaction yielded $\mathsf{U}^{3+}/\mathsf{total}$ U ratios of 0.55 to 0.7 regardless of whether copper or nickel containers

TABLE 4.10. REDUCTION OF NaF-LiF-UF ${}_{4}$ WITH 100% EXCESS URANIUM IN ALL-COPPER APPARATUS AT ${750}^{ \circ  }\mathrm{C}$   

<table><tr><td colspan="2">Analysis of Filtrate (wt %)</td><td rowspan="2">Theoretical* U3+ (wt %)</td><td rowspan="2">Ratio of U3+ to Total U</td></tr><tr><td>U3+</td><td>Total U</td></tr><tr><td>24.9</td><td>26.6</td><td>27.1</td><td>0.93</td></tr><tr><td>24.5</td><td>25.0</td><td>25.2</td><td>0.98</td></tr></table>

*Calculated by assuming complete reduction of UF<sub>4</sub> with U°.

and filters were used. Examination of the interior of the copper containers used in these experiments showed the existence of a mirrorlike metallic coating on the copper that had been in contact with the melt. The thickness of the coating appeared to increase with time and temperature; x-ray diffraction examination of this material showed it to be a mixture of copper and $\mathsf{UCu}_5$ .

The copper in the uraniferous alloy oxidizes quite rapidly in contact with moist air. Tamished specimens showed the presence of copper oxides, in addition to $\mathsf{UCu}_{5}$ , but no $\mathsf{UO}_2$ lines could be found in the x-ray diffraction pattern.

Alloying of this type was also observed when NaF-KF-LiF mixtures were heated with either $\mathsf{U}\mathsf{F}_3$ or with metallic uranium. In both cases, analysis of the filtrate obtained at the end of the equilibration period showed the presence of both $\mathsf{U}^{3+}$ and $\mathsf{U}^{4+}$ in the melt; disproportionation of $\mathsf{U}\mathsf{F}_3$ seems to have been involved in the formation of the $\mathsf{UCu}_5$ alloy. No alloying was observed when only $\mathsf{U}\mathsf{F}_4$ was present in the melt.

No adequate explanation has been found, to date, for the fact that alloying of uranium with copper does not occur with NaF-LiF-UF<sub>3</sub> mixtures or with BeF<sub>2</sub> melts containing UF<sub>3</sub>. Experiments are continuing in an effort to account for the peculiar behavior of the KF-containing melts.

# Reaction of Uranium Metal with Alkali Fluoride Mixtures

B. H. Clampitt C. J. Barton  
Materials Chemistry Division

The reaction of metallic uranium with the NaF-KF-LiF eutectic at high temperatures, reported previously,22 has been shown to occur at $650^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$

a similar reaction occurs at $750^{\circ}C$ when the NaF-LiF eutectic is used. In the recent experiments the purified salt mixture and metallic uranium were placed in a nickel apparatus, and, after formation and decomposition of $\mathsf{UH}_3$ to subdivide the uranium metal, the temperature was raised to the desired value. The contents of the apparatus were agitated by sparging with helium. An air-cooled condenser maintained at slightly above room temperature was included in the exit gas line to condense any alkali metal or alkali fluoride swept out by the flowing helium. The trapped material was dissolved in water and analyzed chemically for potassium, sodium, lithium, fluorine, and total alkalinity.

The results obtained with the NaF-KF-LiF eutectic and with the NaF-LiF eutectic are presented in Table 4.11. It is obvious from the data that most of the trapped material was alkali metal; less than $5\%$ of the condensate was alkali fluoride. Also, both the quantity of alkali metal evolved and the uranium content of the melt increased with time in a quite regular manner. As was expected, the reaction produced more $\mathsf{K}^{\circ}$ than $\mathsf{Na}^{\circ}$ (when both were constituents of the melt) and more $\mathsf{Na}^{\circ}$ than $\mathsf{Li}^{\circ}$ . However, the amount of alkali metal collected did not, by a large factor, correspond to the amount of uranium oxidized. It appears certain that some condensation of the alkali metals occurred in cooler portions of the apparatus before the condenser was reached. The apparatus is being remodeled to eliminate this possibility and to demonstrate the stoichiometry of the process.

The presence of $\mathsf{UF}_4$ in these melts suggests that reactions of the type

$$
\mathrm {U F} _ {3} + \mathrm {K F} \longrightarrow \mathrm {U F} _ {4} + \mathrm {K} ^ {\circ}
$$

occur under these nonequilibrium conditions. The improved apparatus will be used to evaluate this possibility.

D. G. Hill S. Cantor  
Materials Chemistry Division

Studies of the reactions between molten fluorides and metals have included some exploratory work on the equilibrium between sodium-potassium alloy

*Since considerable amounts of alkali fluorides were found in the trapped material, the results of the analyses are not accurate enough for reporting; however, it is felt that the contaminants did not affect the total alkalinity determination appreciably.

and NaF-KF melts at $800^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ . Samples of purified potassium metal and NaF were loaded into a hydrogen-fired nickel capsule in a helium atmosphere. After a welded capsule had been held at $800^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ for several hours, it was quenched and then opened under an atmosphere of helium. Samples of the metal phase were easy to obtain in that they could be poured without difficulty into a test tube having a rubber stopper and a stopcock. A good sample of the salt phase was much more difficult to obtain, because droplets of metal clung tenaciously to it.

A more certain separation of the phases was obtained by evacuating sealed test tubes that had been filled in a helium atmosphere with samples of each phase and allowing the evacuated tubes to partially fill with anhydrous isopropyl alcohol. The alcohol reacted with the metal but not with the salt. Since the salts were insoluble in the isopropyl alcohol, the alcohol fraction was filtered and analyzed to give an approximation of the $\mathrm{Na}^{\circ} / \mathrm{K}^{\circ}$ ratio, and the residue was dissolved

in water to give a water fraction that was analyzed for the NaF/KF ratio. Results for the one experiment which was carried to completion are presented in Table 4.12.

On the assumption that quenching effectively froze the $800^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ equilibrium and that no significant exchange occurred during the short exposure of the salt to isopropyl alcohol, the results in Table 4.12 lead to an equilibrium constant, based on mole fractions of salt in the salt phase and metal in the metal phase, of

$$
K _ {x} = \frac {[ N a ]}{[ K ]} \frac {[ K F ]}{[ N a F ]} = 0. 2.
$$

This compares with the value of 0.29 obtained by Rinck²³ and the value of $0.5 \pm 0.3$ based on standard free energy estimates and ideal activities. It was noted that the salt associated with the metal phase, presumably that salt which was dissolved in the metal at $800^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ , contained much

TABLE 4.12. ANALYSES FOR THE EQUILIBRIUM $\mathrm{K} + \mathrm{{NaF}} \rightleftharpoons  \mathrm{{KF}} + \mathrm{{Na}}$   

<table><tr><td></td><td colspan="2">Metal Phase (meq)</td><td colspan="2">Salt Phase (meq)</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>Na</td><td>K</td><td>Na</td><td>K</td></tr><tr><td>Alcohol fraction</td><td>1.67</td><td>3.42</td><td>0.26</td><td>0.52</td></tr><tr><td>Water fraction</td><td>1.78</td><td>0.05</td><td>3.22</td><td>1.36</td></tr></table>

more NaF than KF; this probably reflects a weakness in the sampling, the separation, or the analysis, since KF has been shown to be ten times $^{24}$ more soluble in $K^{\circ}$ than NaF is in Na $^{\circ}$ . The ratio of salts in the metal phase did not affect the value of $K_{x'}$ since the salt ratio was based on analysis of the water fraction of the salt phase.

# PRODUCTION OF PURIFIED FLUORIDES

G. J. Nessle

F. F. Blankenship

G.M.Watson

Materials Chemistry Division

# Recovery of Contaminated Fuel for Re-use

J. E. Eorgan

J.P.Blakely

Materials Chemistry Division

Fuel used for testing reactor components at ORNL and at Pratt & Whitney Aircraft is expected to become available for reprocessing and re-use at the rate of 2000 to 3000 lb per month. If this material is discharged from the testing equipment in a manner that will minimize oxidation and hydrolysis, the major contaminant will be $\mathsf{CrF}_2$ . If the $\mathsf{CrF}_2$ can be removed before the material is charged to the routine processing cycle, it is felt that reclaiming the material for re-use would be feasible. The present high price of $\mathsf{ZrF}_4$ makes reclamation economically attractive.

The feasibility of removing $\mathrm{CrF}_2$ from NaF $\mathrm{ZrF}_4$ - $\mathrm{UF}_4$ mixtures by treatment of the melt with metallic zirconium and subsequent filtration has been demonstrated on a 5-lb scale, as described below. Accordingly, apparatus is being assembled for treating 750 to 1000 lb of reclaimed fuel with zirconium metal and for filtration of the reduced melt directly into the 250-lb production apparatus.

# Removal of $\mathsf{CrF}_2$ from NaF-ZrF4-UF4 Mixtures

F. L. Daley

Materials Chemistry Division

The standard hydrofluorination-hydrogenation method for the purification of fluoride mixtures will remove $\mathsf{NiF}_2$ and $\mathsf{FeF}_2$ by reduction of these materials to the metals, but it is largely ineffective in removing $\mathsf{CrF}_2$ . Accordingly, if the materials charged to the processing plant contain appreciable concentrations of $\mathsf{CrF}_2$ , an additional processing step must be included. Since the raw materials $\mathsf{NaF}$ , $\mathsf{ZrF}_4$ , and $\mathsf{UF}_4$ contain only traces of $\mathsf{CrF}_2$ , this contamination is not a problem in the production of virgin fuel. However, the fuel materials that will be available for reprocessing after having been used in engineering test units at ORNL and at Pratt & Whitney will contain $\mathsf{CrF}_2$ as the major contaminant.

If the used material is stored in contact with air, considerable amounts of $\mathsf{ZrO}_2$ and/or $\mathsf{ZrOF}_2$ will be formed by hydrolysis when it is reheated, and these compounds could also cause trouble in the reprocessing steps. It is possible, however, to handle and to store the material so that such hydrolysis is negligibly small. In that event the only contaminant present that would not be removed by the normal processing procedure would be the $\mathsf{CrF}_2$ , which would probably be present to the extent of 500 to 1500 ppm chromium.

To achieve nearly quantitative reduction of $\mathsf{CrF}_2$ to $\mathsf{Cr}^{\circ}$ , a considerable fraction of the $\mathsf{UF}_4$ must be reduced to $\mathsf{UF}_3$ . Since the solubility of $\mathsf{UF}_3$ in NaF-ZrF $_4$ melts is relatively low, even at $800^{\circ}\mathsf{C}$ , the amount of reductant must be closely controlled. If this reduction is performed in the standard production equipment, the subsequent hydrofluorination merely reoxidizes the $\mathsf{UF}_3$ to $\mathsf{UF}_4$ and redissolves the $\mathsf{Cr}^{\circ}$ as $\mathsf{CrF}_2$ ; accordingly, the reduction must be accomplished in auxiliary equipment, and the reduced melt must be filtered into the production equipment.

Two experiments were performed on 3-kg batches of purified NaF-ZrF $_4$ -UF $_4$ mixture to test the feasibility of these operations on oxide-free material. The batches consisted of NaF-ZrF $_4$ -UF $_4$ (50.8-45.2-4 mole %) mixed and contaminated with 2 wt % CrF $_2$ (11,500 ppm Cr $^{+ + }$ ) and zirconium metal chips of the desired weight. For the reduction step the charges were placed in unlined nickel reactors, heated, held for 3 hr at $800^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$

with continuous helium sparging, and then transferred through filters into standard hydrofluorination equipment. The hydrofluorination step and subsequent hydrogen reduction were carried out in graphite-lined nickel receivers.

For each experiment, two samples were submitted for analysis. One represented the reduced and filtered material; the other sample was representative of the same salt after hydrofluorination and hydrogenation. The results of the analyses are summarized in Table 4.13.

These results indicate that, in the absence of oxyfluorides and oxides, high concentrations of $\mathsf{Cr}^{+ + }$ may be substantially reduced from molten NaF-ZrF4-UF4 mixtures by the addition of zirconium metal. Furthermore, the loss of uranium because of the insolubility of UF3 is negligible. As was shown previously,25 the concentration of $\mathsf{Cr}^{+ + }$ remaining in the filtrate is a function of the ratio of U3+/U4+ obtained by the particular excess of zirconium used. The concentrations of $\mathsf{Cr}^{+ + }$ obtained in the two experiments are lower than those predicted from equilibrium calculations, for which a $K_{x}$ of $4\times 10^{-4}$ was used, if the CrO is assumed to be at unit activity. This assumption may not be justified in view of the possibility of the formation of chromium-zirconium compounds. Sufficient zirconium to produce 1.0 mole % UF3 reduced the $\mathsf{Cr}^{+ + }$ concentration to 100 ppm, with

negligible uranium loss. This amount of zirconium appears to be adequate for similar NaF-ZrF4-UF4 mixtures.

# Effect of $\mathsf{ZrO}_2$ in Fuel Preparation

F. L. Daley

Materials Chemistry Division

Since $\mathsf{ZrO}_2$ is expected to be a common contaminant in commercial $\mathsf{ZrF}_4$ , studies have been made in order to ascertain the efficiency of conversion of $\mathsf{ZrO}_2$ to $\mathsf{ZrF}_4$ in the present equipment. Appropriate 5-lb mixtures of $\mathsf{NaF-ZrF}_4$ - $\mathsf{ZrO}_2$ - $\mathsf{UF}_4$ which would yield, after complete hydrofluorination, 50 mole % NaF, 46 mole % $\mathsf{ZrF}_4$ , and 4 mole % $\mathsf{UF}_4$ were prepared. A maximum of 10 wt % $\mathsf{ZrO}_2$ was used. The samples were hydrofluorinated in the usual manner at $800^{\circ}\mathsf{C}$ , and the exit gases were periodically sampled by absorption of $\mathsf{H}_2\mathsf{O}$ and HF in pyridine. The resulting liquid was analyzed for fluoride and water. These analyses, though rather erratic during early stages of treatment, indicated that the hydrofluorination efficiency was not greater than $20\%$ with the agitation possible in the equipment used.

If only $20\%$ efficiency were obtained in the production plant, a 50-hr hydrofluorination period would be required for complete conversion of a 100-kg batch containing $10\% \mathrm{ZrO}_2$ . Such a prolonged period is quite impractical. It is obvious that $\mathrm{ZrO}_2$ contamination in the $\mathrm{ZrF}_4$ must be kept to a low figure if high throughput of fuel that meets specifications is to be maintained.

Initial Mixture: Total U, 8.63 wt % (4 mole %); Cr++ (ppm), 11,500

# Pilot-Scale Purification Operations

J. P. Blakely

J. Truitt

C. R. Croft

W. T. Ward

Materials Chemistry Division

Fifty-three batches totaling approximately 500 lb of purified fluorides were processed in the pilot-scale equipment during the quarter. This material, including 22 batches of $\mathsf{BeF}_2$ -bearing compositions, was made up entirely of special batches for use in physical property determinations, phase equilibrium studies, and other programs requiring small batches of fluoride mixtures of possible interest as fuels. The study of processing techniques for the purification of $\mathsf{BeF}_2$ -bearing compositions has continued to indicate that further refinements will be necessary before consistently high-quality material can be produced. The $\mathsf{BeF}_2$ used as charge material has been found to contain carbonaceous occlusions, which are believed to contribute to the high sulfur content of the material. The relation between carbon and sulfur content in some typical, finished batches is shown in the following:

<table><tr><td>Carbon (wt %)</td><td>Sulfur (ppm)</td></tr><tr><td>0.10</td><td>208</td></tr><tr><td>0.37</td><td>460</td></tr><tr><td>0.27</td><td>523</td></tr><tr><td>0.25</td><td>435</td></tr><tr><td>0.05</td><td>20</td></tr><tr><td>0.01</td><td>7</td></tr><tr><td>0.01</td><td>74</td></tr><tr><td>0.01</td><td>5</td></tr><tr><td>0.01</td><td>15</td></tr></table>

Since sulfur in amounts of over 100 ppm is known to be very detrimental to the life expectancy of the processing equipment, it may be seen that rigid specifications must be met before any large-scale processing of $\mathsf{BeF}_2$ -bearing fuels can be undertaken.

# Production-Scale Operations

J. E. Eorgan

J.P.Blakely

Materials Chemistry Division

The large-scale (250-lb batch) fluoride-processing facility continued in operation for approximately one-half of this quarter. Because of an acute shortage of $\mathsf{ZrF}_{4}$ , operations ceased during July to allow stockpiling of a sufficient supply to per

mit full-time production. Operation was resumed in mid-August, and a total of 6960 lb of purified fluoride compositions was produced during the quarter. It has been shown that the facility can produce 1000 lb of processed fluorides per week. No attempts have been made to exceed this rate because of the inadequate supply of $\mathsf{ZrF}_4$ .

Five of the 28 batches processed during this period were the NaF-ZrF $_4$ (50-50 mole %) that was required as the fuel carrier for the ART high-temperature critical experiment. The impurity analyses given in Table 4.14 show that the material was acceptable for use.

# Preparation of $\mathbf{ZrF}_4$

A. J. Gully

Materials Chemistry Division

Zirconium tetrafluoride has been obtained in the past from the Y-12 Production Division, which has operated a facility for converting $\mathsf{ZrCl}_4$ to $\mathsf{ZrF}_4$ at a maximum rate of 250 lb per week. While most of the demands for processed fluorides have been met thus far, it appears that a source of supply capable of producing 400 to 600 lb of $\mathsf{ZrF}_4$ per week will be necessary.

Pilot-scale apparatus for converting $\mathsf{ZrCl}_4$ to $\mathsf{ZrF}_4$ by direct hydrofluorination at a relatively low temperature (200 to $300^{\circ}\mathsf{F}$ ) has been designed, built, and tested. Since analyses of the product from this equipment have made the process appear to be very attractive, a unit capable of yielding 1000 lb of $\mathsf{ZrF}_4$ per week has been designed, and fabrication of the equipment has begun. This facility is expected to be in operation by mid-September 1955. Experimental operations are being

continued in the small-scale equipment to determine optimum operating conditions.

Various private vendors have also been contacted as possible suppliers of suitable material. To date, only one vendor has submitted samples which appear to be reasonably promising, and he has been requested to submit a larger sample, for actual charging to small-scale processing equipment to verify that the material will yield a suitable final product. Tests of this material will be made in the near future. At present, it is not possible to state whether purchased $\mathsf{ZrF}_4$ will be more economical than material prepared at ORNL from hafnium-free $\mathsf{ZrCl}_4$ .

# Batching and Dispensing Operations

J.P.Blakely F.A.Doss

Materials Chemistry Division

Approximately 100 batches totaling 4129 lb of various fluoride compositions were dispensed in batch sizes ranging from 1 to 250 lb. Operational difficulties plus a downward revision in estimated usage of processed fluorides in the ANP program by both ORNL and off-area consumers have, it appears, postponed the time of maximum fluoride consumption to October-November of this year. With presently known testing schedules, an average demand of between 4000 and 4500 lb of processed fluorides per month should prevail during the fiscal year 1956.

A substantial gain in stock inventory was made during the quarter, despite the fact that the inadequate $\mathsf{ZrF}_4$ supply curtailed production by several thousand pounds. A material balance for this quarter follows:

<table><tr><td></td><td>Pounds</td></tr><tr><td>Material on hand at beginning of quarter</td><td>3,818</td></tr><tr><td>Production during quarter</td><td>6,961</td></tr><tr><td>Total</td><td>10,779</td></tr><tr><td>Material dispensed during quarter</td><td>4,129</td></tr><tr><td>Material on hand at end of quarter</td><td>6,650</td></tr></table>

Pratt & Whitney Aircraft reduced their original request for 4750 lb to 1150 lb of processed fluorides for this period; however, to prevent a possible shortage of material for ORNL-ANP usage while the $\mathsf{ZrF}_4$ supply problem is being resolved, only 750 lb of material was shipped to them. Other off-area shipments included 44 lb to Wright Air

Development Center and 73 lb to Battelle Memorial Institute.

Successful attempts to fill four 50-lb cans simultaneously from a 250-lb batch were made, and the practice was extended to smaller batches. The time required to perform these operations was, accordingly, reduced.

# Loading and Draining Operations

N.V. Smith

Materials Chemistry Division

The operations necessary for filling, draining, and sampling of charge material in all test equipment other than the thermal-convection loops have continued at a rate comparable to that of the previous quarter. Over $50\%$ of the operations have involved the handling of alkali metals.

With the rapid increase in testing of alkali metals, consideration of safe, rapid methods of disposal of used metals became necessary. Commercial vendors will not accept the metals for reprocessing. The small-scale methods of disposal formerly used have become inadequate for the quantities now involved. Information obtained from the Mine Safety Appliances Co. led to the installation of an underwater jet through which NaK is forced at 30 psi under 10 ft of water in the disposal quarry. It is now possible to dispose of NaK at a rate of about 30 lb/min. With proper heating of the lines leading to the jet, this installation could be used for sodium disposal.

The ART high-temperature critical assembly was loaded with approximately 750 lb of NaF-ZrF $_4$ (50-50 mole %) and sufficient NaF-UF $_4$ (66.7-33.3 mole %) to reach criticality. Most of the NaF-UF $_4$ was added by helium pressure transfer of the molten material in approximately 5-lb increments. The final titration to criticality was made by using the enriching system designed by the ARE Division for the experiment; the enriching system had previously been filled with fuel concentrate. After each significant addition of Na $_2$ UF $_6$ , the sump was sampled; the uranium content was determined by the ANP Analytical Chemistry Group.

# Enriched Fuel Preparations

J.P.Blakely F.A.Doss

J.E. Eorgan

Materials Chemistry Division

The relocation and installation of processing equipment suitable for preparation of enriched

fuel batches was completed. Two batches of NaF-UF $_4$ (66.7-33.3 mole %) containing enriched uranium were prepared for use in loading of the ART high-temperature critical assembly; four batches of material remaining from the ARE were

tained in recrystallized alumina crucibles; electrical contact between the half cells was effected by a porous bridge of $\mathsf{ZrO}_2$ impregnated with the NaF-ZrF4 mixture.

Attempts have been made to examine cells of the type

$$
M \left| M F _ {2 (s a t d)}, N a F _ {(a _ {1})}, Z r F _ {4 (a _ {2})} \right| \left\| \begin{array}{c} Z r O _ {2} \\ N a F - Z r F _ {4} \end{array} \right\| \left. N a F _ {(a _ {1} ^ {1})}, Z r F _ {4 (a _ {2} ^ {1})}, M ^ {1} F _ {2 (s a t d)} \right| M ^ {1},
$$

also included in the stock available for this experiment.

One preparation of $\mathsf{NaF - ZrF_4 - UF_4}$ (53.5-40-6.5 mole %) was subdivided into three small batches; one of these was transferred into the first in-pile loop to be sent to the MTR for testing. A batch of $\mathsf{NaF - ZrF_4 - UF_4}$ (63.0-25.0-12.0 mole %) to be used in other radiation damage studies was also processed.

# FUNDAMENTAL CHEMISTRY OF FUSED SALTS

# Solubility of Xenon in Molten Salts

R.F.Newton

Research Director's Division

Recent experiments have shown that the gas stripped from fused salts and measured as xenon was contaminated with $\mathsf{SiF}_4$ and with some organic material that was volatile at room temperature but trapped in liquid nitrogen. Accordingly, the previously reported26 value of about $10^{-7}$ mole of xenon per cubic centimeter of solvent for the solubility of xenon in the NaF-LiF-KF eutectic is too high.

The organic contaminant is presumed to arise from stopcock grease. The apparatus is being modified to substitute mercury cutoff traps for the stopcocks. A barium oxide trap is being incorporated to eliminate contamination from the silicon tetrafluoride.

# EMF Measurements in Fused Salts

L.E.Topol

Materials Chemistry Division

Measurements of the electromotive forces of cells in which NaF-ZrF $_4$ (53-47 mole %) is used as the solvent were continued in the temperature range 550 to $700^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ . In all cases, a helium atmosphere was maintained over the half cells con

where $M$ and $M^{\dagger}$ are $\mathbf{Cr}^{\circ}$ , $\mathbf{Fe}^{\circ}$ , and $\mathbf{Ni}^{\circ}$ . These cells appear to be reversible, and they are quite reproducible. However, interpretation of the data from these cells is complicated because the solid phase in equilibrium with themelt is not the simple metal fluoride; evidence reported in a previous portion of this section suggests strongly that complex compounds of which $\mathbf{NiF}_2\cdot \mathbf{ZrF}_4$ is typical are formed. Accordingly, when $M$ and $M^{\dagger}$ are both $\mathbf{Cr}^{\circ}$ , for example, and when the two half cells contain $\mathbf{CrF}_2$ in different amounts, both sufficient to saturate the solution at temperature, small but reproducible and significant potentials are observed. For example, when one half cell contains $7.8\%$ and the other contains $12.6\%$ $\mathbf{CrF}_2$ (solubility of $\mathbf{CrF}_2$ at $700^{\circ}\mathbf{C}$ is $5.8\%$ ), the emf of the cell varies from $15\mathrm{mv}$ at $550^{\circ}\mathbf{C}$ to $8\mathrm{mv}$ at $700^{\circ}\mathbf{C}$ . This potential arises because the $\mathbf{CrF}_2$ added in excess of the saturation concentration "precipitates" $\mathbf{ZrF}_4$ and thus changes the composition of the solvent and affects the activities of the $\mathbf{ZrF}_4$ and of the NaF to a different extent in the two half cells.

Potentials measured at various temperatures for cells of this type are shown in Table 4.15. It may be noted that the sum of values for the $\mathsf{Cr}^{\circ}$ Fe and the $\mathsf{Fe}^{\circ}$ -Ni cells agrees reasonably well at all temperatures with the values for the $\mathsf{Cr}^{\circ}$ Ni cell. If the solid phase in equilibrium with the melt were the simple fluoride $MF_{2}$ and if Brewer's estimates of free energy of formation are assumed to be correct, $E_0$ values of 0.35, 0.25, and 0.60 v are obtained for the $\mathsf{Cr}^{\circ}$ -Fe, $\mathsf{Fe}^{\circ}$ -Ni, and $\mathsf{Cr}^{\circ}$ -Ni cells; these values should be nearly independent of temperature over this interval.

Reproducible emf values have been obtained for cells of the type

$$
\mathrm {C r} ^ {\circ} \left| \mathrm {C r F} _ {2 (c _ {1})}, \mathrm {N a F} _ {(a _ {1})}, \mathrm {Z r F} _ {4 (a _ {2})} \right| \left\| \begin{array}{l} \mathrm {Z r O} _ {2} \\ \mathrm {N a F - Z r F} _ {4} \end{array} \right. \left\| \mathrm {N a F} _ {(a _ {1} ^ {1})}, \mathrm {Z r F} _ {4 (a _ {2} ^ {1})}, \mathrm {C r F} _ {2 (c _ {2})} \right| \mathrm {C r} ^ {\circ},
$$

where $c_{1}$ is less than the saturation concentration of $\mathsf{CrF}_2$ and $c_{2}$ is more. Data for three such cells for which $c_{1}$ was 0.90 wt % $\mathsf{CrF}_2$ and $c_{2}$ had the values shown are presented in Table 4.16.

Additional studies of similar, but less complex, cells of this general type are to be made. While a partial interpretation of the available data would be possible by making use of several assumptions,

additional experimental effort appears to be desirable before such evaluation and interpretation are attempted.

A number of cells were run to determine the feasibility of employing a platinum wire as an electrode for half cells containing mixtures of $\mathsf{FeF}_2$ and $\mathsf{FeF}_3$ in NaF-ZrF. These cells, as

$$
M \left| M F _ {2 (s a t d)}, N a F _ {(a _ {1}) ^ {\prime}} Z r F _ {4 (a _ {2})} \right| \left\| \begin{array}{l} Z r O _ {2} \\ N a F - Z r F _ {4} \end{array} \right\| \left. N a F _ {(a _ {1}) ^ {\prime}} Z r F _ {4 (a _ {2}) ^ {\prime}} M ^ {1} F _ {2 (s a t d)} \right| M ^ {1}
$$

well as others, such as

$$
P t \left| \begin{array}{l l} C r F _ {2} & \\ C r F _ {3} & \text {a n d} P t \end{array} \right| \begin{array}{l} U F _ {3} \\ U F _ {4} \end{array} ,
